REPUBLIC OF UZBEKISTAN
WATER SUPPLY, SANITATION AND HEALTH PROJECT
ANNEX B
B. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
A. Outline Procedure for Processing ICB Contracts
(Kizketken Pilot Project as an example)
Contract Negotiations
. World Bank (WB) publishes a Specific Procurement Notice (SPN) for the proposed contract
in Business Development.
2. Planning and Engineering Design Consultant (PED) prepares the tender design and standard
bidding documents to WB requirements.
3. The Project Implementation Unit (PIU) prepares a list of bidders which have responded
positively to the SPN and sends the bidding documents and list of bidders to the WB for its
"no objection" (NO).
4. The WB notifies the PIU of its NO or its conditional NO subject to recommended revisions
to the bidding documents.
5. The PIU makes the revisions and sends the bidding documents to the list of bidders.
6. On the specific date of bid opening, all bids that have been receive are opened by the PIU
according to the procedures outlined in the WB Procurement Guidelines.
7. The PIU arranges for the bids to be evaluated by a Bid Evaluation Committee consisting of
representatives of Goskomprognostat, Ministry of Communal Services, Govermment of
Karakalpakstan, Karakalpakstan Vodokanal (the "Owner") and the PED.
8. The Evaluation Committee makes its evaluation and selects the successful bidder, and the
PIU prepares the evaluation report and sends it to the WB with the proposal from the
successful bidder for its NO.
9. The WB reviews the evaluation report and proposal from the successful bidder and issues its
NO or asks for more information if not satisfied.
10. Once the PIU receives the Bank's NO, it notifies the bidders of the outcome of its evaluation
and invites the successful bidder to begin negotiations on a specific date.
ii. A small negotiation team consisting of Goskomprognostat, Ministry of Communal Services,
Karakalpakstan Vodokanal, and the PED will negotiate the final contract with the successful
bidder. The contract is initiated by Goskomprognostat, the Ministry of Communal Services,
and the Karakalpakstan Vodokanal and signed by the successful bidder.
12. The PIU sends the final contract to the WB for its NO.
13. After the WB NO is received by the PIU, it is signed by Goskomprognostat, the Ministry of
Communal Services and the Karakalpakstan Vodokanal and becomes effective.
14. Copies of the signed contract are submitted by the PIU to the successful bidder, WB,
Goskomprognostat, the Ministry of Communal Services and Karakalpakstan.



ANNEX B                               -73 -
Contract Supervision
1. The contract will be supervised by a small construction supervision team consisting of the
Project Management and Supervision Consulting Engineer (PMS) as head of the team, a
Vodokanal construction supervisor, and Karakalpakstan Government construction
supervision representative. The team will evaluate the quality of construction according to
the specified standards and norms and construction process in confirmation of construction
progress payments. The PMS Engineer will be responsible for resolving any minor disputes
between the contractor and the Owner (Karakalpakstan Vodokanal).
2. The team will carry out regular inspections as specified in the contract and will submit
reports in the PIU on contract implementation. The contractor will submit invoices to the PIU
as specified in the contract. It will be the responsibility of the PIU to verify that the
construction supervision team has approved the quality and quantity of the work performed
by the contractor according to the terms of the contract. If it is satisfied, it will prepare a
disbursement application (along with verification signed by the construction supervision
team) for signature by Goskomprognostat and will forward the disbursement application to
the WB's Loan Disbursement Department through the Resident Mission in Tashkent.
3. When the WB receives the disbursement request, it checks the information against the
contract and if satisfied, transfers the funds to the contractor's bank account as specified in
the disbursement application.



- 74 -                            ANNEX B
B. Proposed'Implementation Arrangements for the Demand-Based Rural Water Supplies
1.    Application or Initial Proposal or Request. Applications, proposals or requests may
come in from different sources: the local administration (e.g., Kolkhoze administration,,
grassroots initiative, implementing agencies, or government administration. The application s
may come in on a 'walk-in basis' or in batches. Registration of the application has to take place in
order to allow for follow-up and monitoring. Also, a confirmation has to be given to the source
of the application.
2.    Check Competence of the Project Owner. Is the application rehabilitation or a new
system?
3.    Check with Eventual Implementing Agencies. A check should be made whether the
location is already soinewhere on the agenda of another agency which have the same or related
fields of work (water supply, sanitation, hygiene education). Forces may be joined or, in the case
of another agency already addressing the project, a decision is to be taken who will take care of
the location. Additionally, a check should be made whether other development efforts are under
way which are not related to water supply. Too much attention from different sources for the
same location should be avoided as it may be confusing and putting too much stress on the
population. This check may lead to the rejection of the project.
4.    Check Water Supply Master Plan. Collect information about the medium and long-
term planning regarding water supply for that location; what kind of water resources are
indicated, main pipeline, ground water, etc.?
5.    Field Visit 1: Reconnaissance Visit made by the Project Team. It is essential to build
up a small, reliable and sufficiently representative bridge head within the project location.
Therefore, it is proposed to establish a Water Users Association (WUA). This WUA needs to be
actively involved from the start. It will represent the community but may also be the local partner
of the project in communications with the community and execution of local assignments. Its
activities would include:
(a)   check application or proposal realism;
(b)   re-check project's competence, based on field situation;
(c)   determine current water use practices and existing supply systems;
(d)   determine the range of technical possibilities; and
(e)   organize and establish a WUA (as the local steering committee).
6.    Collect Additional Required Information. Collect information on the topography,
demographics, health situation, and geo-hydrology.
7.    Decide Whether Reconnaissance Visit is Sufficiently Successful. Outcomes would be,
positive, go ahead with the next steps and activities, rejection of the project, or additional
information or measures are required, decision pending and redo previous steps.
8.    Field Visit 2: Outlining Made by the Project Team. Discuss with groups of men,
women and local leaders water needs, affordability, contributions from the community,
community organization. Offer, discuss and test acceptance of various technical possibilities,
levels of service, cost of these, organizational consequences and measures. Establish a good idea
about the future operating agency (who can in principle become the operator, also alternative
organizational set-ups).



ANNEX B                                -75 -
9.    Generate Variants. Based on the findings of the outlitiing visit, a set of variants should
be drawn-up: a low variant, an intermediate variant, and a high variant (different service
levels/technologies and hence different levels and arrangements of resources and organizations).
The purpose is to generate and elaborate the variants on the technical, economic, social ad
organizational main aspects, arriving at a so called 'functional design.' The functional design
would give the participants in the project a reasonable idea of what they may expect and enable
further decision making. Generating these variants is the primary task of the project team,
assisted by for exatnple the envisioned operator if so required. Indicate or outline of each variant:
(a)   service levels (what type of supply, volume, quality);
(b)   technical systems (hardware);
(c)   investment, operational cost, total cost, least cost;
(d)   organizational setup and measures for operations;
(e)   further activities and work plan for implementation; and
(f)   tasks and responsibilities of the involved parties.
10.   Field Visit 3: Discuss Variants. The variants (function designs) have been discussed
with the representation of the beneficiaries target group as well as the eventual operator(s). This
would include, seeking understanding on the variants, seeking consensus, and deciding whether
(a) there is sufficient consensus and ground to continue elaboration of a variant or alternatively,
(b) to redo the variants step with additional information or (c ) aborting the project
11.   Formal Agreement on the Functional Design. In case of consensus, the plan,
responsibilities and arrangements concerning the preferred variant have been formalized. This
formal agreement is to be drawn up among the project, the target community representatives and
the operator. This formal agreement is to be seen as a joint plan which needs further approval to
proceed with a detailed design.
12.   Seeking Approval with Competent Authorities (Regional Government, Water Utilities,
Goskomprognostat). Does this functional design fit in the whole project framework (financing,
implementing agencies, consultant support, approach, type of technology, etc.)? Is sufficient
financing available for investment and eventual operation? Is there sufficient organizational
capacity to implement this? Negative responses of the competent authorities may require re-
doing certain steps or alternatively, abandoning the project.
13.   Preparation of Detailed Design. Once formal approval has been given for the functional
design, a detailed design can be made. This detailed design would consist of:
(a)    Technical. The traditional technical design and preparation for construction
activities and results that is among other things:
(i)    drawings;
(ii)   bills of quantities;
(iii)  construction and manufacturing instructions;
(iv)   work planning; and
(v)   tender documents.



-76-                             ANNEX B
(b)   Operator's Organization. Setting up and preparation of e organization of the
operator would include:
(i)   organizational design;
(ii)  human resources development (training);
(iii)  budget and budget control systems; and
(iv)  institutional development work plan.
(c)   Community Organization. Activities in order to prepare and organize the
community:
(i)   work plan for community organization (determines who will do what,
when and how);
(ii)  details on information campaign concerning service level, proper use of
water;
(iii)  details on information campaign concerning paying for water, billing and
revenue collection;
(iv)  details on public tap organization (user groups, caretaking arrangements);
and
(v)   details on community contribution during construction (material, labors).
14.   Discussion of Detailed Design. The detailed design has to be discussed with the involved
parties (project, community, operator, authorities) and where necessary, adjusted.
15.   Approval of Detailed Design. Once the whole detailed design package has been
approved, the project would move to the next phase, implementation. The carious technical and
organizational work plans can be elaborated and implemented. The detailed design approval can
be laid down in an agreement among the project implementing agency (and/or operator), the
community, and the competent authorities.
16.   Construction Phase. The activities and sequence during the construction phase would be
determined during the planning phase. Construction phase is not limited to technical activities
regarding the technical infrastructure but would also include organizational and social activities.
The contents of the activities would heavily depend on the outcome of the planning process. It is
therefore not opportune to give a step-by-step process for the construction phase.

-89 -                          ANNEX E
REPUBLIC OF UZBEKISTAN
WATER SUPPLY, SANITATION AND HEALTH PROJECT
ANNEX E
E. ENVIRONMENTAL ANALYSIS
A. Project Impact and Environmental Rating
1.    The Uzbekistan Water Supply, Sanitation, and Health Project is designed to improve the
health and quality of life of the target population by providing safe drinking water and improved
sanitation. The project area, covering the Republic of Karakalpakstan and Khorezm Oblast, has
been facing problems of water salinity and consequent potable water shortages for the past three
decades. The project is intended to implement a series of urgent schemes to improve the viability
and operation of the water supply and sanitation sectors. The overall impact would be a
substantial improvement in the level and quality of service while optimizing energy inputs and
preserving the quality of regional water resources.
2.    The project has been assigned a category B rating. Phase I project activities involve
primarily rehabilitation and modest expansions of the existing pipelines and treatment works.
Most construction activities would be small scale and without excessive noise or need for
explosives. Impacts on traffic flow and problems of noise and dust from construction activities
would be temporary and suitable mitigation measures would be taken to minimize these impacts.
The project has a sewerage component consistent with the water supply schemes to handle any
increases in sewage flow. The project also has a sanitation component integrated with a health
and hygiene education program to enable maximum improvements in public health. Adverse
environmental impacts of the project are therefore insignificant as compared to the overall
benefits.
B. Project Background and Environmental Issues
3.    The Republic of Karakalpakstan and Khorezm Oblast together constitute over one third
of the total land area of Uzbekistan and are irrigated by the Amu Darya river which is the major
surface water source in the area. Intensive agricultural developments relying on major irrigation
schemes and chemical inputs have lead to a severe deterioration in the quality of the regional
water resources over the past few decades.
4.    The major problem is that of water salinity and, except in summer when snow melt
occurs, the salinity levels in most local surface water sources (river and canal water) are found to
be higher than the acceptable level for drinking water. Groundwater reserves are also highly
saline, with levels as high as 25-30 g/l in the deep aquifers. Shallow freshwater lenses in several
areas and extraction of this water to meet local demands, while inadequate in itself, is causing
recharge of the freshwater lenses with the surrounding saline water thereby exacerbating the
regional water quality problems.
5.    Much of the current water demand in the project area is met by a major water supply
scheme which uses water from the Tuyamuyun group of reservoirs located almost 240 km south
of the main settlements in Karakalpakstan. The scheme is extremely energy intensive and
involves pumping water over hundreds of kilometers into the urban and rural areas of



ANNEX E                                  -90-
Karakalpakstan and Khorezm Oblast. The project was launched in the Soviet period when
electricity was highly subsidized and the pumping costs were low. Water was supplied free or at
nominal cost to the consumers. In the present economy the subsidies cannot be afforded and very
soon the consumers would have to pay more for water.
7.    While the costs of water are difficult to meet, the condition of the water supply systems is
universally poor and the quality of service is below expected levels. The distribution systems in
the urban areas are old and corroded leading to frequent incidents of burst pipes (average 6.1
breaks per km in Karakalpakstan in 1995). The systems are therefore operated at less than half
the design pressure which poses a risk of inflow with consequent contamination of the water
supply. Leakage is estimated to be as high as 30 percent and the quantity of water supplied is
much lower than the design supply. Many areas on the outskirts of towns and in upper stories of
apartment blocks do not receive any water in the summer.
8.    At the local distribution centers, the Tuyamuyun water is blended with water from other
local sources (shallow groundwater and surface irrigation canals) to augment the supply. While
the chemical quality of the local sources is unacceptable during certain times of the year, the
water treatment plants are in generally poor condition and face shortages of chemicals for
turbidity removal and disinfection. As a result, the piped water supply does not always meet
bacteriological or chemical water quality standards.
9.   In the project area, fully developed piped sewerage and sewage treatment systems exist
only in the major cities (Nukus, Urgench, Khiva and Druzba) where they are operated and
maintained by the local Vodokanals. In some urban or peri-urban locations there are local
systems covering limited areas. In rural locations, some bigger farms operating food processing
factories have wastewater collection and treatment facilities for trade effluents. In most areas, on-
site sanitation is used extensively and often consists of simple pits which are emptied from time
to time.
10.   The sewerage systems as well as sewage pumping stations are in a state of disrepair and
substantial sections of the existing network require rehabilitation. The treatment plant at Nukus
has been constructed recently and has excess capacity, but the treatment facilities at Urgench,
Khiva and Druzba are overloaded and in a poor state of repair. Toilets in homes and public
buildings are unhygienic, partly because their design and construction are so poor that effective
cleaning is virtually impossible. In many urban and peri-urban areas there are numerous
multistory apartment blocks that are not connected to piped water supply or drainage despite
being equipped with internal pipework and sanitary appurtenances. The overall household
sanitation conditions in the rural areas are very poor as well.
11.   The major impact of existing water supply and sanitation conditions is on public health.
Diarrheal diseases are reported to be the second most important cause of infant mortality in the
project area. Other water related diseases such as hepatitis A, typhoid, and paratyphoid are also
known to constitute major health concerns. Although published health data are not conclusive in
linking health solely to water supply, recognized long-term health indicators show a higher
incidence, compared with other parts of Uzbekistan, of certain kidney ailments that are claimed
to be associated with chemically poor water.



-91-                          ANNEX E
12.   To improve the performance of the water supply and sanitation sectors in the project area,
a number of activities are proposed as part of the Uzbekistan water supply, sanitation, and health
project. The following sections describe these activities along with their positive and negative
impacts.
C. Project Activities and Impacts
Main Pipeline Supply to Urban and Rural Areas
13.   Major project activities and impacts under this component are:
Activities:   Repairs or replacement in Karakalpakstan and Khorezm Oblast of
sections of urban distribution pipes which are prone to failure or have excessive
leakage. It is estimated that 100 km of pipework in Karakalpakstan and 82 km of
pipework in Khorezm Oblast is involved. In addition, the chlorination facilities at
the water distribution centers in Karakalpakstan and Khorezm Oblast would be
rehabilitated.
Impacts:    This component would reduce losses and maintenance costs and
improve water quality by reducing the risk of groundwater inflow to the system. It
would also allow higher pressures to be used in the distribution system, resulting
in continuous supply to more consumers. Provision of chlorination equipment
would improve bacteriological quality of water. Some traffic disruptions would
occur while rehabilitation work is going on but these would be temporary and
would be dealt with by arranging alternative routes where necessary.
Activities:   Rehabilitation of water treatment works involving improvements
in the filtration unit at the 65 Mld capacity plant at Nukus; and provision of spare
parts and filter sand for the 50 Mld capacity plant at Urgench.
Impacts:    These activities would improve the efficiency of turbidity removal
thereby achieving better disinfection and producing better quality water. No
adverse impacts are likely to occur from these activities.
*    Activities:   Exploration and pump testing to develop groundwater sources for
possible capacity expansion from 10 Mld to 25 Mld at the Chalysh groundwater
source; and for providing an additional supply of 20 Mld in Karakalpakstan. This
component might eventually involve construction of additional wells with power
supplies and pipelines to link the source to the main Khorezm Oblast supply
system.
Impacts:    This component would augment the water supply with a cheaper
local water resource. Pumping tests are not likely to have any adverse impacts but
digging new wells may disturb the natural flora and fauna. Care would be taken to
select locations and pipeline routes that minimize these impacts.
*     Activities:   Completion of works started by the Vodokanal on trunk pipelines
linking the towns of Beruni, Turtkul and Kazanketken to the main Tuyamuyun
pipeline.



ANNEX E                                - 92 -
Impacts:    This would allow additional supplies to be provided to the towns
that at present rely on groundwater which is highly seasonal in availability. No
additional negative impacts are likely by the completion of ongoing works.
Activities:   Extension of the trunk pipeline in Khorezm Oblast to connect the
town of Shavat and reinforcement of connections to Koshkupir and Yangi.
Impacts:    This component would augment supplies to a population of 39,000
within the towns and secondary trunk mains off the new pipelines would allow
other rural consumers to be supplied from the main system. The connections to
Shavat would also allow the 10 Mld capacity Shavat groundwater source to be
added to the main pipeline system resources. Laying of pipelines would involve
some digging and trenching and mitigation measures would be taken to minimize
the impacts.
Development of Local Water Supply and Distribution Systems
14.   Major activities and impacts are:
-    Activities:   Exploitation of alternative water supply sources such as hand
pumps, small local desalinization units, fresh groundwater lenses with
chlorination, or other small scale package water treatment plants, and
rehabilitation of existing EKOS desalination units. This component would be
implemented following a demand-driven approach and would involve providing
grants to households for installing hand pumps. Where hand pumps cannot be
installed, community participation would be sought to choose among different
sources and levels of service and the schemes would be implemented through the
Agrovodokanals.
Impacts:.  About 200,000 people in rural settlements of the Republic of
Karakalpakstan and 20,000 people in rural Khorezm Oblast are proposed to be
covered by water supply from these alternative sources. While installation of hand
pumps is not likely to have any significant adverse environmental impacts,
development of fresh groundwater lenses would require judicious sitting and
operation norms to mitigate further deterioration in quality of the groundwater
regime. Operation of desalination units and package water treatment plants would
likewise require adequate disposal of the brine and other waste streams.
*     4Activities:   Construction of a 105 km long pipeline connecting the Kungrad
water treatment works to the town of Muynak.
Impacts:    This component would bring water to an additional 13,700 people
who are at present without an adequate supply of potable water. The laying of the
pipeline would involve some digging and trenching but the area along the pipeline
route is sparsely populated and impacts of dust and noise are not expected to be
substantial. Care would be taken to minimize disturbances to the natural habitat.
Water Demand Management and Loss Reduction
15.   Major activities and impacts are:



-93 -                         ANNEX E
*     Activities:   This component would involve actual measurement of leakage and
wastage, and device and implement a strategy for reducing both to acceptable
levels. This may include active leakage control policies, programs to increase
public awareness, and measures by the Vodokanals and Agrovodokanals to reduce
wastage such as repairing or replacing faulty internal plumbing. Demand
management, in particular the extension of water metering in apartment buildings
and other consumer groups would be examined on a trial basis in pilot areas.
Impacts:    These activities would improve water availability and are not likely
to have any adverse impacts.
Sanitation, Health and Hygiene
16.   The activities proposed under this subcomponent and their likely impacts are:
*     Activities:   This component would provide approximately 7,900 and 6,900
new hand pumps to be installed in Karakalpakstan and Khorezm Oblast to supply
44 percent and 47 percent of the population, respectively. The overall number of
hand pumps would be increased so that there is on average 1 hand pump per 40
people in areas where there is no access to piped supplies. The hand pumps would
be manufactured to an approved design in Uzbekistan and installed by local
contractors such as the PreAralia Expedition in Nukus.
Impacts:    This component would address the urgent need for access to
potable water in rural areas and is not likely to have any adverse environmental
impacts.
*    Activities:   This component would involve construction of improved latrines
and installation of hand washing facilities in individual households and public
facilities such as schools, primary community health care centers, canteens, and
market places. A grant financing scheme has been designed to help households
and communities finance the sanitation facilities.
Impacts:    This component would address the urgent issue of unsanitary
facilities and practices in the project area and is not likely to have any adverse
environmental impacts.
*     Activities:   This subcomponent would cover health and hygiene education of
both the rural and urban populations in the project area.
Impacts:    This program would complement the sanitation subcomponent of
the project and as such is an integral element of the overall strategy for improving
public health. No adverse impacts are likely to occur from these activities.
D. Overall Environmental Impact
17.   The overall impact of the project would be to provide an additional 239,000 urban and
241,000 rural consumers with piped water supply and 220,000 rural consumers with improved
alternative water supplies. In addition, 749,000 urban and 755,000 rural consumers would benefit
from improved levels of service. The entire population is expected to benefit from the health and
hygiene education program, to the extent that this program has an impact on changing hygiene



ANNEX E                                 -94-
practices and on health, especially among children. While providing better water supply and
sanitation facilities, the project would also strengthen the various organizations involved in
public health in the project area, such as,the regional Vodokanals and Agrovodokanals; the
Tuyamuyun-Urgench and Tuyamuyun-Nukus main pipeline operators; the regional Sanitary
Epidemiological Services; and the Regional Centers of Health.
18.   Overall, the project is envisaged to contribute substantially to the improvement of public
health and quality of life in the project area and any transient impacts associated with its
implementation are considered to be comparatively minor.



-95 -                           ANNEX F
REPUBLIC OF UZBEKISTAN
WATER SUPPLY, SANITATION AND HEALTH PROJECT
ANNEX F
F. WATER RESOURCES AND WATER QUALITY
1.    The only water source in the project area is the Amu Darya river, which drains a large
part of southern Central Asia. The total area of the basin is about one million square kilometers.
The Republic of Karakalpakstan and Khorezm Oblast lie in the downstream end of the Amu
Darya basin, where the annual precipitation is about 420 mm in steppe areas and 170 mm in
desert areas. Most of the precipitation occurs during winter in the formn of snow.
2.    Substantial withdrawals are made at various points along the Amu Darya river. The
largest extractions take place upstream of the project area and are subject to international
agreements. By far, agriculture is the largest user in the basin and accounts for about 70 percent
of total withdrawals. Drainage water is returned to the river through irrigation drain canals but at
greatly increased salinity levels.
3.    At the beginning of the 1980s, the Amu Darya river was dammed and regulated through
four interlinked reservoirs, which form the Tuyamuyun reservoir system. Apart from supplying
water for irrigation, domestic and industrial purposes, hydropower is generated from the
reservoirs' releases. Table Fl shows the available storage capacity at each reservoir. As can be
seen, the combined maximum live storage of the system is about five million cubic meters.
Table Fl: Storage Capacity of the Tuyamuyun Reservoir System
Top Water   Gross Storage  Surface Area  Minimum Drawoff  Live Storage
Reservoir     Level (m)      (Mcm)         (km2)        Level (m)       (Mcm)
Main Reservoir     130          2,340         303            120            2,070
Kaparas            130            960          70            120             555
Sultansandjar      130          2,690         149            116            1,690
Koshbulak          130          1,810         128            116            1,020
Total                       7,800        650                           5,335
Source: Feasibility Study Report, May 1996
A. Quantity of the Amu Darya River
4.    Table F2 and Figure Fl show annual runoffs in the Amu Darya at various observation
points over a period of 10 years, from 1986-1995. Darganata and Tuyamuyun Gorge are located
immediately upstream and downstream of the Tuyamuyun reservoir system. Samambay and
Kizyldjar are located downstream of the Takhiatash barrage, near Nukus in the delta area of the
Amu Darya. As can be seen from the data, the average annual flow upstream at Darganata during
the analyzed period was about 40,000 Mcm, or five times the combined gross storage capacity of
the system. On average, approximately 75 percent of the Amu Dayra's flow at Darganata reaches
the delta area of Kizyldjar.

5.    As shown by Figures Fl, F2 and F3, there is a great variation between the dry and wet
years and between the wet and the dry seasons. In Kizyldjar, for example, while the average
annual run-off was about 314 cubic meters per second, there were some months when the
minimum runoff flow fell as low as 3.2 cubic meters per second. Limited water resources is a
characteristic of dry years. Thus, particular attention should be given to the operating rules of the
system if the Amu Darya river is used as the primary source of domestic water during drought
periods.

B. Quality of the Amu Darya River
6.    The main pollution sources in the Amu Darya river are the irrigation drainage/collectors
located in the in the Bukhara and Kashikadarya regions. These regions have the largest
agricultural land in the river basin. The large volume of collector/drainage water discharged back
into the system has resulted in a drastic increase in the rate of mineralization (salinization). Water
mineralization increases along the Amu Darya river, reaching the highest values in the lower



ANNEX F                                      -98 -
reaches of the basin. Figures F4 through F7 show maximum, average and minimum monthly
levels of mineralization at four sites in the Amu Darya river between 1974 and 1993.

7.    Uzbekistan's standard for maximum allowable concentration of mineralization is 1,000
mg/liter. With the exception of Termez, the salinization at the three sites exceed the standard.
8.    The highest level of salinity is reached in March-April, while the lowest level is reached
in July. The mineralization concentration shows a seasonal trend, which fluctuates with the
discharge rate. During the summer season, when the discharge rate is high, the mineralization
concentration is low.
9.    The mineralization problem could be addressed by changing the operating rules of the
Tuyamuyun reservoir systems and filling the Kaparas reservoir with better quality water. This is,
however, a very costly alternative as shown below.



ANNEX F                                 -100 -
10.   The design capacity of the Kaparas reservoir is 960 Mcm at an elevation of 130 meters,
which gives a life-storage capacity of 550 Mcm. In order to impound 410 Mcm of water in the
Kaparas reservoir, water must be filled up to a level of 128 m. However, under current operating
rules, Kaparas reservoir reaches the 128 m level between November and December, when water
quality has badly deteriorated. Therefore, it is impossible to fill the reservoir with better quality
water by gravity flow.
C. Groundwater Resources
11.   Groundwater resources can be found in abundant quantities in the project area. One key
question regarding their development, however, relates to their quality. Fresh groundwater fields
are closely connected to the surface water. The decrease of flow rate in the Amu Darya river and
the rise in level of salinity during the last decades have negatively impacted on the quality of
groundwater, especially on shallow groundwater since it is recharged either directly or indirectly
by water from the Amu Darya.
12.   Under the current operating practices of extraction, shallow groundwater quality is being
deteriorated. When water is extracted in September, poor-quality surface water is pulled into the
aquifer leading to a steady decline in overall quality. However, if shallow groundwater aquifers
are exploited to their maximum during June to September, when surface water of reasonable
quality is available, then good quality water can be drawn into the aquifers. In the remainder of
the year, shallow aquifers should not be used to preserve their quality. This source then can be
used to meet peak demands during the months of June to August.
13.   A  recent study has estimated that under a proper operating extraction system
accompanied by quality protection measures, potential resources of relatively fresh shallow
groundwater may amount to some 94.9 Mcm in Karakalpakstan and about 98.6 Mcm in Khorezm
Oblast. Current extractions represent 7 percent and 2 percent of these amounts, respectively.
14.   Groundwater is also found in the project area in a 200-300 m thick deep artesian aquifer
at 100-300 meters below sea level. The deep groundwater aquifer is free of chemicals and
bacteriological contamination, but the level of salinization is relatively high, ranging from 1,000-
5,000 mg/liter in the east to 20,000-30,000 mg/liter in the west.



-101-                          ANNEX G
REPUBLIC OF UZBEKISTAN
WATER SUPPLY, SANITATION AND HEALTH PROJECT
ANNEX G
G. PROJECTED WATER DEMAND
1.    Demand for piped water depends on several factors, namely, population growth, per
capita consumption, volume-based pricing of water supply, coverage and levels of services,
household income level, industrial output, and demand management programs. Production of
piped water, in turn, is determined by the level of efficiency or unaccounted-for water.
2.    According to recent population statistics, the 1995 population totaled 1.35 million
inhabitants in Karakalpakstan Republic (632,700 in urban areas and 722,000 in rural areas) and
1.26 million inhabitants in Khorezm Oblast (363,300 in urban areas and 899,700 in rural areas).
The Government forecasts a population expansion from 2.6 million to approximately 3.9 million
between 1995 and 2015. Population projections up to year 2015 are presented in Table GI, and
are based on Government forecasts.

3.    The feasibility study consultant assessed two scenarios for future water demand. The first
scenario or Plan I assumes that average service levels in urban areas of Karakalpakstan and
Khorezm Oblast would increase respectively from 76 percent and 86 percent in 1995 to 100
percent in both areas by the year 2010. In rural areas, the level of piped water services would
increase from 41 percent and 51 percent to 85 percent and 90 percent by the year 2010 and to 100
percent in both areas by the year 2015. The second scenario or Plan 2 assumes a more modest
expansion of services in rural areas of Karakalpakstan and Khorezm Oblast, from 41 percent and
51 percent to 55 percent and 65 percent by the year 2010 and to 60 percent and 70 percent by the
year 2015.

percent increase. Industrial and commercial future demands were also based on modified supply

9.    Regarding metering, the government expects to continue with its policy of individual

shown in Table G6. About domestic consumers, the government strategy is to introduce bulk
metering and individual metering in a piloting basis.

the project area, these increases would have to be implemented over a relatively long period and

11.   The results of the analysis are shown in Table G8 and Figures Gl and G2. The estimates
under a new pricing policy, combined with a rigorous unaccounted-for water reduction program
and an active water conservation campaign show that total water demand by the year 2015 would
represent 1.5 times the 1995 demand level. Losses would experience a reduction of 17 percent
between 1995 and 2015. Total level of production, in turn, would increase at an annual rate of 1
percent from 624 Mid to 769 Mld. If no measures are taken to manage demand or reduce waste,
production level by the year 2015 would have to rise by 2.8 percent per year in order to match
increase in income, coverage and industrial growth. Figure G3 presents the balance between
available supply and water production.
