-116-                              ANNEX I
REPUBLIC OF UZBEKISTAN
WATER SUPPLY, SANITATION AND HEALTH PROJECT
ANNEX I
I. ANALYSIS OF OPERATIONS OF EXISTING WATER PRODUCTION AND
DISTRIBUTION AGENCIES
A. Institutional Setup
1.    Water and sewerage services in Uzbekistan and Karakalpakstan are being provided
through a number of agencies. All urban potable water and sewerage services in Uzbekistan fall
on the national level within the portfolio of the Ministry of Communal Services (MCS). Two
separate departments of this ministry, one for the municipal water and sewerage agencies (the
Vodokanals) and one for the inter-regional trunk pipeline agencies (such as the one for the bulk
water producers of the Tuyamuyun reservoir) are in charge of overall supervision and
management, control, sector planning and regulatory aspects. Water and sewerage services in the
rural areas in Uzbekistan fall within the jurisdiction of the Ministry of Agriculture (MoA) which
oversees and coordinates the activities of the regional rural water agencies (Agrovodokanals)
through its department of Agrovodokanals. The set-up is similar in the Republic of
Karakalpakstan where its Ministry of Communal Services and Ministry of Agriculture oversee
directly the operations of the regional municipal water and sewerage agency (Vodokanal) and the
regional rural water agency (Agrovodokanal) there.
2.   In the past the central authorities in Tashkent exercised a strong control of all activities
associated with the production and distribution of water. This was modified recently with
delegating the responsibility for overseeing the distribution of water to the provincial level,
which in the project area is the Khorezm Oblast. The central government is still overseeing all
aspects associated with the treatment and transport of surface water from large reservoirs
including its delivery to the distributing agencies.
3.    The State Planning Authority (Goskomprognostat) plays a key role in the review and
approval of all investment in the water supply and sewerage sector. Execution of major capital
works is entrusted to specific commissions/agencies, for instance in the case of the Aral Sea
region, the Aral Sea Regional Commission (Priaraliye).
4.    Branch offices of the Ministry of Communal Services of Uzbekistan are part of the
administration of the provinces (Khakimiat), and referred to as the Territorial Communal
Services Amalgamations (TCMA) as they cover also gas distribution and urban transport,
oversee under the close supervision by the deputy governor the regional municipal water and
sewerage agencies, the Vodokanals. Rural water agencies in Uzbekistan, the Agrovodokanals,
are also controlled by the provincial authority through the deputy governor with assistance from
the MoA.



ANNEX I                              -117-
5.    This section therefore provides an overview of the operations of the following
institutions:
Khorezm Oblast:
(a) Tuyamuyun-Urgench;
(b) Vodokanal Khorezm; and
(c) Agrovodokanal Khorezm.
Republic of Karakalpakstan:
(a) Tuyamuyun-Nukus;
(b) Vodokanal Karakalpakstan; and
(c) Agrovodokanal Karakalpakstan.
The following comments refer to the operations of all of the above listed agencies.
B. Oversight
6.   In line with administrative policies of the Govermment of Uzbekistan the territorial
administration in each province (Khakimiat) is headed by the governor (Khakim), who is
appointed by the President of the Republic of Uzbekistan. In this capacity he exerts a strong
control of all aspects of public life including the daily operation of the government agencies,
state owned enterprises and utilities. In discharging his duties, the governor is assisted by the
deputy governor (deputy Khakim) and the local representation of key ministries, like the
Ministry of Finance, and the respective overseeing ministries, which for the Vodokanal Khorezm
and the Tuyamuyun-Urgench bulk supply is the Ministry of Communal Services, and for the
Agrovodokanal Khorezm, the Ministry of Agriculture.
7.   In the Republic of Karakalpakstan the set-up reflects the fact that the government is much
closer to its agencies. Tuyamuyun-Nukus which is administratively responsible to the Ministry of
Communal Services in Tashkent liaises closely with the Ministry of Communal Services in
Karakalpakstan in particular its advisory board, which includes the three deputy ministers and the
heads of all enterprises. The daily operations of Vodokanal Karakalpakstan are controlled by the
Ministry of Communal Services of Karakalpakstan exerts a strong control of the daily operations
of Vodokanal Karakalpakstan. A deputy minister within the Ministry of Communal Services of
Karakalpakstan supervises the technical and financial operations of Vodokanal Karakalpakstan.
In addition, the activities of Vodokanal Karakalpakstan are reviewed by the advisory board of the
Ministry of Communal Services of Karakalpakstan. Agrovodokanal Karakalpakstan is controlled
by the Ministry of Agriculture of Karakalpakstan. Both agencies are controlled by the Ministry
of Finance in Karakalpakstan and in Tashkent. While the first one concentrates its controls on all
operational aspects, the control by the latter ones is restricted to the financial aspects.
C. Vehicles and Telecommunication Equipment
8.   In general the about 70 percent to 80 percent of the vehicles of each agency are in a state
of more or less readiness. The average age is about 6 years which may require replacement of a
portion of the fleet as regular preventive maintenance was not perforned on a regular basis. With
the exception of Agrovodokanal Khorezm which uses the telephone, all other agencies have their



-118 -                             ANNEX I
branch offices connected by radio. However there are often problems in keeping in daily radio
contact.
D. Inventories
9.    As all agencies suffer from chronic cash shortages, inventories were drastically depleted
for performing repairs and urgent maintenance. Stocks, as required by normal operations, are
therefore not kept at normal levels. Where they exist, inventories comprise some electrical
switches, and uniforms. Only items that would deteriorate in a short time are in protective
custody. Larger parts, such as pumps, which were acquired some time ago are stored outside on
the soil lacking any kind of protection. However none of the agencies had a supply of pipes of
any diameter or fittings.
E. Tariffs
10.   The tariff policy of the bulk water supply agencies is different from the one pursued by
the water distributing agencies. While the tariffs of Tuyamuyun-Urgench and Tuyamuyun-Nukus
are highly subsidized by the central government to compensate for the pumping costs, only
Agrovodokanal Karakalpakstan is still receiving some limited subsidy. In general, the tariff
policy of the distributors is to achieve covering of the apparent cost of operation, maintenance
and nominal depreciation. This is not, however, the case if regular preventive maintenance and
replacement of worn-out equipment is considered. Depreciation charges are very low as they are
based on historic cost.
11.   Changes in the electricity tariff, increases in the cost of chemicals and wages require tariff
adjustments. In order to secure a tariff increase, each agency prepares studies for the future tariff
levels of category II and III customers (government agencies, commercial establishments,
industries and large farms). In the Khorezm Oblast the resulting proposal is then reviewed by the
department of price setting within the regional finance representation (local branch of the
Ministry of Finance) and once approved by it, is endorsed by the governor. Tariff adjustments for
Tuyamuyun-Urgench follow similar procedures with review by the department of price setting
within the regional finance representation (local branch of the Ministry of Finance), subsequent
review by the governor prior to the approval by the Minister of Communal Services.
12.   Tariffs for water sales to domestic users are set directly by the governor.
13.   Specific user groups such as veterans, teachers and persons of the medical profession
have preferential tariffs. Veterans pay only 50 percent. Fifty percent of the water bill for teachers
and persons of the medical profession are paid for by the central government out of the budgets
of the respective ministries.
14.  In Karakalpakstan Tuyamuyun-Nukus' request for tariff adjustments is first reviewed by
the department of price setting within its Ministry of Finance and after review by its Ministry of
Communal Services and approved by the Minister of Communal Services. Requests for tariff
adjustments for Vodokanal Karakalpakstan are first reviewed by the Ministry of Communal
Services of Karakalpakstan, then the antimonopoly commission of the Council of Ministers of
Karakalpakstan. They become effective upon approval by the Council of Ministers of
Karakalpakstan. Tariffs for water sales to domestic users are set directly by the Council of
Ministers of Karakalpakstan. Tariff adjustments for Vodokanal Karakalpakstan are first reviewed



ANNEX 1                               -119-
by the Ministry of Agriculture of Karakalpakstan and then the department of price setting within
the Ministry of Finance in Karakalpakstan and Tashkent and finally approved by the Minister of
Agriculture in Karakalpakstan in order to become effective. Tariffs for water sales to domestic
users are set directly by the Minister of Agriculture.
F. Billing and Collection
15.   All agencies bill their customers on a monthly basis. Large consumers (category II and
category III) enter into a yearly customers sales agreement, which specifies the applicable tariff.
Approximately 85 percent of the large customers accounts are metered and are read monthly.
Customers without a meter pay a monthly rate determined according to the diameter of the
supply line or according to the hours of pumping. This system is also used when the meter is out
of order. Bills are prepared manually and payments are posted manually. In the case of
Vodokanal Khorezm sales and billing data is transferred to a recently established computer-based
customer data base. This database has still to be modified to permit its use as an account
management tool including an age analysis of accounts, and also to be the backbone of a
computer-based billing system. Where sewerage charges are levied, the water bill includes the
bill for sewerage services as a separate line. A 17 percent value added tax is added to the water
and sewerage charges for category II and III customers and transferred to the government when
the bill is settled.
16.   While bills to category II and III customers are settled through the banking system,
domestic customers pay their monthly rate on the basis of a monthly coupon in the annual
customer booklet, either at the head office or directly to the bill collector. Large customers
occasionally use promissory notes issued by the Ministry of Light Industries to cotton producers.
Vodokanal Khorezm has instituted a system that in case of overdue bills assures their payment.
In line with the provision of the sales agreement, Vodokanal Khorezm sends payment
instructions for the water bills directly to the bank, who makes the transfer, once there are funds
in the account of customer. Arrears by category III are high because of insolvency of customers
and because of the harvest cycle which provides the farms only once a year with income. In order
to overcome the problems caused by infrequent payment of water bills by the large farms,
Agrovodokanal-Khorezm customers use regularly promissory notes, which are issued two times
per year by the Ministry of Light Industry to the cotton growers. These notes come in different
denominations and are used for payment of supplies, water and energy.
G. Accounting and Reporting
17.   The GoU adopted in 1994 a new accounting system. The system presently used by the
agencies is, however, an earlier modification of the former USSR system. It is a double entry
system, and accounting is performed on a cash basis and not on an accrual one. This accounting
system does not provide the information necessary for managing the agencies. Accounts are
manually kept and no plans exist for computerization in the near future. The accounts of all
agencies are kept separate from the Government accounts.
18.   All agencies prepare a number of reports at regular monthly, quarterly and annual
intervals. However the information provided is more used as statistical reference and for control
purposes than to assist in managing the operations.



-120 -                            ANNEX I
H. Audit
19.   The economic unit within each of the agencies prepares regular reports on the operations
by identifying costs and revenues during a certain period in order to compare the actual results
with the planning data. Quarterly reports on the operational and financial results are submitted by
the Tuyamuyun bulk suppliers to the Ministry of Communal Services and Ministry of Finance.
Both the Ministry of Communal Services and Ministry of Finance through its revenue service
audit annually the accounts of the two Tuyamuyun bulk. Vodokanal Khorezm  and
Agrovodokanal Khorezm submit quarterly reports on the operational and financial results to the
deputy governor's office and the regional services of the respective ministry and the Ministry of
Finance. The latter one, through its revenue service annual audits of their accounts which
concentrate on the proper use of government funds.
20.   The following institutions listed below, which all have accounts separate from the
Government, would be involved in the project.
I. Tuyamuyun-Urgench (Tuyamuyun-Ur)
Overview
21.   Tuyamuyun-Ur is responsible for the production and delivery of water as bulk supplier to
the water distribution agencies serving mainly the province (Oblast) of Khorezm (Vodokanal
Khorezm and Agrovodokanal Khorezm) and to some small centers located on the left bank of the
Amu Darya river in the Republic of Karakalpakstan (Vodokanal Karakalpakstan and
Agrovodokanal Karakalpakstan). Tuyamnuyun-Ur is a government budget organization within the
Ministry of Communal Services as it receives subsidies from the government budget. Its
accounts are separate from the government accounts.
22.   Construction works are executed by the republican department for the inter-regional
water mains. Upon completion of the works they are transferred to Tuyamuyun-Ur for operation
and maintenance and are included as assets in its balance sheet.
23.   Tuyamuyun-Ur's customers are the two Vodokanals and the two Agrovodokanals.
Operations are assured through its head office and include accounting, billing/collection and
finance as well as technical support to the treatment plant, the two pumping stations, and the
distribution centers.
Management
24.   Tuyamuyun-Ur's director is in charge of routine day-to-day operations with a regular
supervision by the deputy governor (Khakim). The director is appointed by the minister of
communal services upon proposal by the governor. His office is, however, not involved in
Tuyamuyun-Ur's procurement process.
25.   The organizational structure is that of an agency with no specific responsibility for long-
term capacity planning. Under the director the chief engineer, who is also the deputy director and
the chief accountant, different units are in charge of production, operations, accounting,
procurement, economics, internal control, and worker safety. An inter-ministerial commission is
responsible for long-range capacity planning and has established in the past long-term
development plans covering the period from 1990 to 2015.



ANNEX I                              -121-
Personnel
26.   Tuyamuyun-Ur has a staff of about 734 out of which 52 are assigned to the head-office.
The other staff man the pumping and-distribution stations as well as the treatment plant. Salary
and wage scales are determined by the Ministry of Communal Services on the basis of the
government-wide salary and wage structure.
Billing and Collection
27.   Payment by the Vodokanal and Agrovodokanal is slow and arrears amounted in June '96
to Sum 7 million. Although the government is paying regularly its subsidy, occasional problems
were encountered in the timely payment of salaries and wages.
Procurement
28.   Goods and services are mostly procured through the Ministry of Communal Services on
the basis of a needs assessment transmitted at the beginning of the year. Tuyamuyun-Ur pays
upon delivery of goods the equivalent in local currency. No shortage in the supply of chemicals
and coagulants were experienced recently. Barter agreements do not exist any longer.
Financial Aspects
29.   Tuyamuyun-Ur does not cover its operations and maintenance costs. Over the last three
years Tuyamuyun-Ur has consistently received subsidies from the government covering some 70
percent of the cost of its operations. Arrears from its customers have adversely affected the
financial management of Tuyamuyun-Ur, however not as much as the water distributing
agencies, since the government subsidy is paid regularly. The arrears, which amounted to some
Sum 7 million at the end of May '96, have constrained Vodokanal-Khorezm's operations such
that workers have occasionally not been paid regularly. Maintenance of essential plant is still
being performed on a regular basis, however routine maintenance and repair is being postponed.
Tuyamuyun-Ur has never used any credit facilities from the banking system. In mid '96 Sum 9
million was payable to the electricity corporation.
J. Vodokanal Khorezm (VK-Khorezm)
Overview
30.   VK-Khorezm is responsible for water distribution in the urban areas in the province
(Oblast) of Khorezm. It receives its water from the regional bulk supplier Tuyamuyun-Ur (70
percent) and 30 percent from its own wells. VK-Khorezm is a financially self-sufficient
government organization within the Ministry of Communal Services and its accounts are
therefore separate from the government accoui Ls.
31.   Prior to the take over of construction by the Ministry of Communal Services, VK-
Khorezm was responsible for executing all its construction activities. Upon completion of works,
VK-Khorezm becomes responsible for their operation and maintenance and the assets are
transferred to VK-Khorezm and included in its accounts. Construction works are executed by the
Directorate of Rural Construction in the Aral Sea Region, attached to the governor's (Khakim)
office. The funds are included in the government budget upon approval by the Government
Planning Office (Goskomprognostat). A recently issued Presidential Decree foresees the
installation of meters by all users to control waste. Meters are installed at the cost to the customer



-122 -                             ANNEX I
and recuperated through monthly charges. As VK-Khorezm has to prefinance the acquisition of
the meters, pipes fittings and the required labor, execution of this program has been extremely
slow due to insufficient funds.
32.   Operations are assured through 10 branch offices in the districts (rayons) and with one
serving the city of Urgench. All accounting, billing/collection and financial operations are
centralized in the head office with the exception of the branch in Druzhba, which performs all
operations by itself and reports to the head office.
Management
33.   The organizational set up reflects the need of an organization primarily concerned with
operating an existing distribution system. Under the director the chief engineer, who is also the
deputy director and the chief accountant, supervise the different divisions that are in charge of
production, operations, accounting, customer relations, procurement, economics, internal control,
and worker safety. As a water distributor, VK-Khorezm is not actively involved in the medium
and long-term planning process of securing water resources and large distribution network
extensions. An inter-ministerial commission is responsible for long-range capacity planning and
has established in the past long-term development plans covering the period from 1990 to 2015.
Personnnel
34.   VK-Khorezm has a staff of about 997 out of which 25 are assigned to the head-office.
Another 20 employees work in the central repair shop. The remainder staff serves in the 11
branch offices, which cover a district (rayon). Salary and wage scales are determined by the
Ministry of Communal Services on the basis of the government-wide salary and wage structure.
Procurement
35.   Goods and services with the exception of imported materials (mostly chemicals) are
procured locally. Items involving foreign exchange are procured through the Ministry of
Communal Services on the basis of a needs assessment transmitted at the beginning of the year.
VK-Khorezm pays upon delivery of goods the equivalent in local currency. In case the imported
items are available locally at a lower price they are procured locally and a promissory note with a
bank guarantee is given. Shortage of local currency has sometimes resulted in serious shortages
of vital supplies. Barter agreements with large customer for settling of their water bills are only
used for items for direct use by VK-Khorezm such as pipes and concrete construction materials.
Terms of the barter is covered in the sales agreement.
Financial Aspects
36.   VK-Khorezm is financially self-sufficient for operations and maintenance only. Over the
last three years VK-Khorezm has not received any subsidy from the government. Arrears from
large customers, in particular cooperative farms, present a serious problem in the financial
management of VK-Khorezm. The arrears, which amounted to some Sum 26 million at the end
of May '96, have constrained VK-Khorezm's operations such that workers have not been paid on
occasion for periods of up to two months and that only the most urgent repairs have been
performed. Also the absolute minimum of chemicals and spare parts are procured, and regular
maintenance does not take place. How long this situation can persist is difficult to project,



ANNEX I                               -123 -
however the already deteriorated state of equipment and plant would require the execution of a
major rehabilitation program soon.
37.   So far VK-Khorezm has not used bank credits because of the high interest rate. In mid
'96 Sum 26 million was payable to the electricity corporation.
K. Agrovodokanal Khorezm (AK-Khorezm)
Overview
38.   AK-Khorezm is the water distributor in charge of the rural water system in the Khorezm
Oblast. It receives about 96 percent of the water from the bulk supplier Tuyamuyun and 4 percent
from own sources. AK-Khorezm is under the Ministry of Agriculture and locally controlled by
the representation of this ministry as well as the local representation of the Ministry of
Communal Services. Recently some discussions have taken place to transfer all the
Agrovodokanal regional centers in the Republic of Uzbekistan from the Ministry of Agriculture
to the Ministry of Communal Services in an effort to have all potable water agencies under one
ministry. However no decision has been made as the Minister of Agriculture opposes this
transfer. AK-Khorezm is a financially self-sufficient government organization within the
Ministry of Agriculture and its accounts are therefore separate from the government accounts.
Operations are grouped in 11 branch offices which are responsible for their daily operations
including billing, collecting and accounting. According to the director, the head office finances
its operations through an assessment of 4 percent of the net income of the branch offices after
taxes (profit tax of 38 percent). This has still to be verified as the system of accounts specifically
foresees head-office expenses as a cost allocated to operations.
39.   AK-Khorezm was previously responsible for executing all its construction activities. This
has changed and the Ministry of Communal Services executes now all major construction work
for AK-Khorezm. Upon completion of the works, the assets are transferred to AK-Khorezm and
included in its accounts. AK-Khorezm's own construction works is therefore limited to the
execution of system expansion lines.
Management
40.   AK-Khorezm's director is in charge of routine day-to-day operations but is supervised by
the deputy governor (Khakim). The director and the departments heads are appointed by the
deputy governor. The deputy governor also approves AK-Khorezm's financial plans and budgets.
His office is, however, not involved in AK-Khorezm's procurement process.
41.   The organizational set up reflects the need of an organization concerned with operating
the water distribution systems that are spread out across the whole province. The branch offices
have therefore a higher degree of independence that those of AK-Khorezm and the head office
perceives its role more that of a coordinating and control unit than that of one actively involved
in the daily operations of a far flung operation. Under the director the chief engineer, who is also
the deputy director and the chief accountant, different divisions are coordinating, controlling and
advising the branch office operations in the area of production, operations, accounting, customer
relations. Procurement, statistical analysis including operational control, security and worker
safety are administered) centrally. Long-range capacity is the responsibility of an inter-ministerial



-124 -                            ANNEX I
commission that has established in the past long-term development plans covering the period
from 1990 to 2015.
Personnel
42.   AK-Khorezm has a staff of about 500 out of which 12 are assigned to the head-office.
Another 20 employees work in the central repair shop. The remainder staff serves in the 11
branch offices, which cover a district (rayon). Salary and wage scales are determined by the
Ministry of Communal Services on the basis of the government-wide salary and wage structure
Procurement
43.   Goods and services with the exception of imported materials (mostly chemicals) are
procured locally. Items involving foreign exchange used to be procured through the
government's central procurement agency in Tashkent. The change-over in the economy has
brought about some difficulties in the timely procurement of necessary items such as chemicals.
Shortage of local currency has sometimes resulted in serious shortages of vital supplies. AK has
no barter agreements with its customers for settling their water bills.
Financial Aspects
44.   AK-Khorezm is financially self-sufficient for operations and maintenance only. Over the
last three years AK-Khorezm has not received any subsidy from the government. Arrears from
large customers, in particular cooperative farms present a serious problem in the financial
management of AK-Khorezm. The absolute minimum of chemicals and spare parts are procured,
and regular maintenance does not take place. How long this situation can persist is difficult to
project, however the already deteriorated state of equipment and plant would require the
execution of a major rehabilitation program soon. Bank credits have not been used since the
interest rates are too high at 150 percent.
L. Tuyamuyun-Nukus (Tuyamuyun-Nk)
Overview
45.   Tuyamuyun-Nk is responsible for the production and delivery of water as bulk supplier to
the water distribution agencies serving the Republic of Karakalpakstan (Vodokanal
Karakalpakstan and Agrovodokanal Karakalpakstan). Tuyamuyun-Nk is a government budget
organization within the Ministry of Communal Services as it receives subsidies from the
government budget. Its accounts are separate from the government accounts.
46.   Construction works are executed by the republican department for the inter-regional
water mains. Works are upon completion transferred to Tuyamuyun-Nk for operation and
maintenance and are included as assets in its balance sheet.
47.   Tuyamuyun-Nk's customers are Vodokanal and Agrovodokanal. Operations are assured
through its head office including accounting, billing/collection and finance as well as technical
and operational and support to its pumping stations and treatment plant.
Management
48.   Tuyamuyun-Nk's director is in charge of routine day-to-day operations with regular
coordination with the Ministry of Communal Services in Karakalpakstan. The director is



ANNEX 1                              -125 -
appointed by the minister of communal services in Tashkent. His deputy is appointed by the
department overseeing the regional water supply agencies of the Ministry of Communal Services
in Tashkent. All department heads are appointed by the director. The Ministry of Communal
Services in Tashkent is only in so far involved in Tuyamuyun-Nk's procurement process as it
procures chemicals and other supplies through its central procurement unit.
49.   Tuyamuyun-Nk's organizational set up corresponds to the needs of a bulk water producer
and supplier with no specific responsibility for long-term capacity planning. Under the director
the chief engineer, who is also the deputy director and the chief accountant, different units are in
charge of production, operations, accounting, procurement, economics, internal control, and
worker safety. An inter-ministerial commission is responsible for long-range capacity planning
and has established in the past long-term development plans covering the period from 1990 to
2015.
Personnel
50.   Tuyamuyun-Nk has a staff of about,710 out of which 52 are assigned to the head-office.
The other staff man the pumping and distribution stations as well as the treatment plant. Salary
and wage scales are determined by the Ministry of Communal Services on the basis of the
government-wide salary and wage structure
Equipment
51.   Tuyamuyun-Nk's laboratory needs specialized equipment to perform all water analysis.
Its dispatching center is inoperable because the installation is incomplete and critical items are
needed. Equipment lists for the laboratory and the dispatch center were handed over to the
mission.
Procurement
52.   Goods and services are procured through the Ministry of Communal Services on the basis
of a needs assessment transmitted at the beginning of the year. Tuyamuyun-Nk pays upon
delivery of goods the equivalent in local currency. The local procurement of chemicals is
difficult as chemicals and coagulants are not available in sufficient quantities. Barter agreements
do not exist.
Financial Aspects
53.   Tuyamuyun-Nk is not financially self-sufficient for operations and maintenance. Over the
last three years Tuyamuyun-Nk has consistently received subsidies from the government
covering some 70 percent of the cost of its operations. Arrears from its customers have adversely
affected the financial management of Tuyamuyun-Nk, however not as much as the water
distributing agencies, since the government subsidy is paid regularly. The arrears, which
amounted to some Sum 12 million at the end of May '96, have constrained VK-Khorezm's
operations such that the electricity bill cannot be paid on time. Timely payment of wages is a
continued problem as the banks lack the necessary cash when needed. Maintenance of essential
plant operations are still being performed on a regular basis, however routine maintenance and
repair is being postponed.



-126 -                            ANNEX I
54.   Tuyamuyun-Nk has never used any credit facilities from the banking system. In mid '96
Sum 9 million was payable to the electricity corporation.
M. Vodokanal Karakalpakstan (VK-KK)
Overview
55.   VK-KK is responsible for water distribution in the urban areas in the sovereign the
Republic of Karakalpakstan. It receives 55 percent of its water from the regional bulk supplier
Tuyamuyun-Nukus and 45 percent from its own wells and surface water. VK-KK is a financially
self-sufficient government organization within the Ministry of Communal Services of
Karakalpakstan and its accounts are therefore separate from the government accounts.
56.   The Ministry of Communal Services of Karakalpakstan through its department of capital
construction is in charge of all construction works. Upon completion of the works, the assets are
transferred to VK-KK and included in its accounts. The funds are included in the government
budget and are allocated through the Ministry of Communal Services of Karakalpakstan for the
different municipal services such as water supply, heating and provision of gas. The planning
process is complex. VK-KK prepares a proposal, which is then reviewed by a layer of
government agencies starting with the Ministry of Communal Services of Karakalpakstan,
followed by the Council of Ministers of Karakalpakstan, the submitted to Goskomprognostat in
Tashkent, and finally approved by the Council of Ministers in Tashkent. While the general
supervision of works is performed by the Ministry of Communal Services of Karakalpakstan, the
construction contract is signed by VK-KK, which also makes the necessary payments upon
receipt of the funds through the Ministry of Finance in Tashkent, the Ministry of Finance in
Karakalpakstan, the Ministry of Communal Services of Karakalpakstan and by VK-KK's bank.
As the financing is tied, the bank controls the proper usage of funds.
57.   Operations are assured through 17 branch offices in the districts (rayons) and with one
serving the city of Urgench. All accounting, billing/collection and financial operations are
centralized in the head office.
Management
58.   VK-KK's director is in charge of routine day-to-day operations but with close supervision
by the deputy minister of the Ministry of Communal Services of Karakalpakstan. The director is
appointed by the minister of communal services of Karakalpakstan and who also approves the
appointment of the deputy director the chief engineer and the chief accountant. The deputy
minister also approves VK-KK's financial plans and budgets. His office is, however, not
involved in VK-KK's procurement process.
59.   The organizational set up reflects the needs of an organization primarily concerned with
operating the existing distribution system concentrated in the capital, Nukus and in 17 branch
offices. Under the director the chief engineer, who is also the deputy director and the chief
accountant, different divisions are in charge of production, operations, accounting, customer
relations, procurement, economics, internal control, and worker safety. As a water distributor, it
is not actively involved in the medium and long-term planning process of securing water
resources and large distribution network extensions. An inter-ministerial commission is



ANNEX I                               -127-
responsible for long-range capacity planning and has established in the past long-term
development plans covering the period from 1990 to 2015.
Personnel
60.   VK-KK has a staff of about 1,100 out of which 25 are assigned to the head-office.
Another 20 employees work in the central repair shop. The remainder staff serves in the 17
branch offices, which cover a district (rayon). Salary and wage scales are determined by the
Ministry of Communal Services on the basis of the government-wide salary and wage structure
Insurance
61.  Insurance exists only as third-party liability coverage for the operation of its vehicle.
Workmen compensation is assured through disability pensions from trade unions. As everybody
contributes to the trade union, also because of the social benefits in the form of free meals,
vacation camps, pension fund, all workers are covered for accidents. There is no need perceived
for any other type of insurance.
Procurement
62.   Goods and services with the exception of imported materials (mostly chemicals) are
procured locally. Items involving foreign exchange are procured through the Ministry of
Communal Services in Tashkent on the basis of a needs assessment transmitted at the beginning
of the year. VK-KK pays upon delivery of goods the equivalent in local currency. In case the
imported items are available locally at a lower price, they are procured locally and a promissory
note with a bank guarantee is given. Shortage of local currency has resulted in serious shortages
of vital supplies. Barter agreements with large customer for settling of their water bills do not
exist, nor are promissory notes used. The central procurement service of VK-KK also purchases
fuel in bulk. Coupons are then used by the branch offices to obtain the necessary quantities.
Financial Aspects
63.   Arrears from large customers, in particular cooperative farms and government budget
organizations present a serious problem in the financial management of VK-KK. The absolute
minimum of chemicals and spare parts arc procured, and regular maintenance does not take
place.
64.   VK-KK has never used credit facilities from the banking system because of the excessive
high rate of interest. In mid '96 Sum 26 million was payable to the electricity corporation.
N. Agrovodokanal Karakalpakstan (AK-KK)
Overview
65.   AK-KK is the water distributor in charge of the rural water system in the Republic of
Karakalpakstan. It receives about 13 percent of the water from the bulk supplier Tuyamuyun and
87 percent from its own surface and ground sources, which include some 300 desalination plants.
AK-KK distributes its water either through meter connections to collective farm, which
themselves redistribute the water to the population living on the farm or directly to government
agencies, commercial establishments and some domestic house connections. AK-KK is under the
Ministry of Agriculture of Karakalpakstan. AK-KK is a budget organization since it receives



-128 -                             ANNEX I
subsidies for covering its operating and maintenance expenses from the government. Its accounts
are, however, separate from the government accounts. Operations are grouped in 11 branch
offices, which serve the 15 administrative regions (rayons) and which are responsible for their
daily operations including billing.
66.   AK-KK was previously responsible for executing all its construction activities. This was
modified by entrusting execution of all major construction for AK-KK to the Directorate of Rural
Construction in the Aral Sea Region. Upon completion of the works, the assets are transferred to
AK-KK and included in its accounts. AK-KK's own construction works is therefore now limited
to the execution of system expansion lines which nevertheless cover the installation of some 200
km of pipelines.
Management
67.   AK-KK's director is in charge of routine day-to-day operations. He is appointed by the
Minister of Agriculture of Karakalpakstan and reports to him on a regular basis. Departments
heads are appointed by the director. The Minister of Agriculture also approves AK-KK's
financial plans and budgets which cover production forecasts, forecast of operating expenses, and
level of tariffs. His office is, however, not involved in AK-KK's procurement process.
68.   The organizational set up corresponds to the needs of an organization that has to mange
water delivery systems in a widely spread area. The branch offices have therefore a higher degree
of independence that those of VK-KK and the head office is more for controlling and
coordinating the activities of the branch offices than directly managing them on a routine basis.
Under the director the chief engineer, who is also the deputy director and the chief accountant,
different units are coordinating, controlling and advising the branch offices' operations in the
area of production, operations, water quality control, accounting, personnel, worker safety,
customer relations. Procurement is administered centrally with distribution from the head office
which also includes the supply of fuel which is purchased in bulk and then distributed by AK-
KK's own tanker trucks to the branch offices. Long-range capacity is the responsibility of an
inter-ministerial commission that has established in the past long-term development plans
covering the period from 1990 to 2015.
Personnel
69.   AK-KK has a staff of about 930 out of which 28 are assigned to the head-office, which
also includes 30 mechanics and 19 drivers. The remainder staff serves in the 11 branch offices,
which cover a district (rayon). Salary and wage scales are deternined by the Ministry of
Agriculture on the basis of the government-wide salary and wage structure.
Procurement
70.   Goods and services with the exception of imported materials (mostly chemicals) are
procured locally. Items involving foreign exchange used to be procured through the
government's central procurement agency in Tashkent. However the recent change-over in the
economy has created difficulties in the timely procurement of necessary items such as chemicals.
Shortage of local currency has contributed to this resulted in serious shortages of vital supplies.
AK-KK has no barter agreements with its customers for settling their water bills.



ANNEX I                               -129-
Financial Aspects
71.   AK-KK has so far achieved only limited financially self-sufficiency for operations and
maintenance. Over the last three years AK-KK has continued to receive subsidies from the
government. Arrears from large customers, in particular cooperative farms present a serious
problem in the financial management of AK-KK. The arrears, which amounted to some Sum 1
million at the end of May '96, have constrained AK-KK's operations such that only the most
urgent repairs were performed. Although the government advances to the farms twice a year
funds for the purchase of fertilizer, equipment and fuel, these funds are severely limited and
cannot be used to pay for the water bill. The absolute minimum of chemicals and spare parts are
procured, and regular maintenance does not take place.
72.   AK-KK has not used any credit facilities from the banking system because of the
prohibitively high interest rates which vary between 90 percent and 150 percent.

REPUBLIC OF UZBEKISTAN
WATER SUPPLY, SANITATION AND HEALTH PROJECT
ANNEX J
J. FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
A. Introduction
1.    The limited and occasionally inconclusive and contradictory financial and operational
data available within the six agencies (Tuyamuyun-Khorezm, Vodokanal-Khorezm,
Agrovodokanal-Khorezm,  Tuyamuyun-Karakalpakstan,  Vodokanal-Karakalpakstan   and
Agrovodokanal-Karakalpakstan) does not permit a thorough financial analysis. Nevertheless the
data were used in preparing pro forma income statements, balance sheet and funds flow
statement for determining under which conditions a phasing out of the operating subsidies to the
bulk water supplier would be possible. Without tariff increases that go beyond adjustments for
inflation, subsidies would have to be foreseen for the near future, however, at reduced levels if
the operating efficiency of the distributing agencies could be improved and their "savings" be
used to compensate for part of these subsidies, which is summarized in Table J 1.
B. Assumptions for Financial Projections
Basic Assumptions
2.    The annual local and international inflation rates were assumed to be 4.0 percent and 2.4
percent respectively.
Income Statements
3.    Sales projections for the bulk water agencies assume that their level of production would
not be increased given the high cost of this source of supply. The demand increases foreseen for
the distributing agencies would be satisfied by the development of local water sources.
4.    Vodokanal-Karakalpakstan. Sales were forecast to increase by 1.6 percent with the
supplies to be increased by 2.5 percent from local sources and UzTransGaz. Other revenue was
assumed to correspond to 1.5 percent of water sales. Production and distribution losses are
forecast to decrease from 25 percent in 1996 to 23 percent by 2006.
5.    Agrovodokanal-Karakalpakstan. Sales were forecast to increase by 3.6 percent with
the supplies to be increased by 4.3 from local sources. Other revenue was assumed to correspond
to 1.5 percent of water sales. Production and distribution losses are forecast to stay steady at 5.0
percent.
6.    Vodokanal-Khorezm. Sales were forecast to increase by 2.0 percent with the supplies to
be increased by 4.1 percent from local sources. Other revenue was assumed to correspond to 1.5
percent of water sales. Production and distribution losses are forecast to decrease from 25 percent
in 1996 to 23 percent by 2006.
7.    Agrovodokanal-Khorezm. Sales were forecast to increase by 3.2 percent with the
supplies to be increased by 12.3 percent from local sources. Other revenue was assumed to



ANNEX J                               -132-
correspond to 1.5 percent of water sales. Production and distribution losses are forecast to stay
steady at 7.0 percent.
C. General Assumptions
8.    The following assumptions were the same for all six agencies (i.e., Tuyamuyun-
Khorezm,  Vodokanal-Khorezm,  Agrovodokanal-Khorezm,  Tuyamuyun-Karakalpakstan,
Vodokanal-Karakalpakstan and Agrovodokanal-Karakalpakstan).
* Water tariffs were adjusted for the forecast period for inflation only.
Costs
* The cost of electricity and consumption of chemicals is based on an analysis of the
requirements for proper operation of the existing facilities and those to be constructed
under the project.
* Expense for maintenance and repair are set at initially below 1.0 percent of the value of
replacement cost of gross fixed assets and growing to 2.3 percent over time.
- Personnel costs are based on actual staffing and costs. A provision was added to allow for
the recruitment of specialized staff, amounting to initially 2.0 percent and decreasing over
time to 0.5 percent.
* Administrative costs are forecast to increase with the growth of production; from year
1997 on, an additional increase by 2.0 percent was assumed. Fuel was assumed to
increase initially by 2.0 percent and from 2000 onwards by 3.0 percent. With the
implementation of the new works, a provision was made for materials which would
increase from 2.0 percent initially to 6.0 percent.
* Depreciation was calculated on revalued gross fixed assets. The provision for renewal has
been adjusted for inflation.
Balance Sheets and Cashflow Statements
*  The balance sheet for 1996 is an estimate.
* Net fixed assets and work in progress have been calculated taking into account projected
completion dates.
* Inventories are gradually increased from one and a half months to six months of
purchases.
*  Accounts receivable are forecast to decrease gradually from about 150 days of net sales
by 1996 to 60 days by 2005.
* A minimum cash balance of roughly one month of operating expenses has been provided
for.
* Accounts payable represent three months of cost of purchases (chemicals, electricity,
supplies for repair and maintenance and renewal investments).

-159-                               ANNEX K
REPUBLIC OF UZBEKISTAN
WATER SUPPLY, SANITATION AND HEALTH PROJECT
ANNEX K
K. ECONOMIC ANALYSIS: METHODOLOGICAL ASPECTS AND RESULTS
1.    This annex presents the economic analysis of the Uzbekistan Water Supply, Sanitation
and Health Project. Apart from presenting the results, it also introduces the methodology
followed in the analysis.
2.    Beneficiaries of the Project. The immediate impacts of the project would be a safe and
reliable water supply services for about 1.12 million people living in rural areas of the Republic
of Karakalpakstan and Khorezm Oblast, and 0.41 million people living in urban areas. The
project would also directly benefit about 50,000 inhabitants in rural and peri-urban areas with
improved sanitation facilities. Table Kl presents a breakdown by region of the population to
benefit from the implementation of the project.
Table Kl: Beneficiaries of the Project
(in '000 inhabitants)

3.    Apart from above impacts, the water supply component would also provide additional
water supply to serve industrial, institutional and commercial users. The additional volume of



ANNEX K                               -160-
piped water to be sold to these consumers by the year 2002 has been estimated at 6.2 million
cubic meters, representing 22 percent of incremental sales volume.
4.    Furthermore, the project would help to strength the institutional capacity of the various
agencies working in the sector aiming at optimizing the use of existing infrastructure and local
water sources, improving planning and project design capacity, increase productivity and
efficiency, and reducing water losses and waste. For example, it is expected that the project
would be able to satisfy an incremental demand of 28.8 million cubic meters of water by year
2005, with an increment in production capacity of only 13.1 million cubic meters. Some
economies of scale are also expected to be gained by the integration of the various agencies into
a single unit. Through the implementation of the demand management component, the proposed
project would encourage water conservation for domestic consumers.
5.    Least-Cost Solution. The project has selected the most economic alternatives to improve
the overall provision of safe water and the environment conditions of urban and rural
populations. Demand management and leakage reduction programs were selected over other
alternatives to increase production capacity. Development of local sources (e.g., groundwater
lenses and irrigation canals) for water production would allow reducing the excessive operational
costs of having to pump water from the Tuyamuyun reservoir. Improved on-site sanitation
facilities were preferred over centralized waterborne sewerage systems in rural and peri-urban
areas.
6.    Economic Costs. The econornic analysis was conducted using the average market rate of
exchange applicable in February 1996, i.e., US$1.00 = Sum 36. Because the total investment
costs of the proposed project are expected to be incurred in foreign exchange (e.g., dollars), no
conversion factors were used for local financial costs. Similarly, no conversion factors were used
for foreign financial costs. It was considered inappropriate to use a shadow exchange rate
different from the market rate. Value added taxes, estimated at 17 percent of project costs, were
excluded from total costs. Physical contingencies estimated at 5 percent (technical assistance)
and 10 percent (non-technical assistance) of project costs were included in the economic costs.
7.    Operating costs were estimated on the basis of current production costs per cubic meter of
water, with adjustments to reflect economies of scale in administration and labor costs due to the
consolidation of the sector and the economic cost of power (US$0.07 per KwH). Average
variable costs per cubic meter of water produced were assumed as follows: US$0.19 for piped
water supply in both urban and rural areas, US$0.67 for potable water from existing desalination
units in the "without project" scenario and $0.56 in the "with project" scenario, and US$0.03 for
water from hand pumps. The average variable cost of piped water reflects the use of local
sources, which would be blended with Tuyamuyun water to satisfy incremental demand.
8.    Economic Benefits. Benefits were assessed for each type of beneficiary. The starting
point for estimating benefits was the "Willingness to Pay" survey carried out under the Social
Needs Assessment in November 1994. This was complemented by the "Water Vendors" survey,
the "Taste Tolerance Survey," and the "Hand Pump" survey carried out during the preparation
phase of the project. Data availability allowed a detailed estimation of benefits for different
beneficiaries.



-161-                                    ANNEX K
9.     Under the "Willingness to Pay" survey, households were asked how much they would
pay for different levels of services. The results revealed that if improved and reliable water
services were available for house connections, yard connections, standposts, and individual hand
pumps,' an average household of six members would be willing to pay US$1.66, US$1.52,
US$1.08, and US$1.112 per month (in February 1996 prices) or 3.8 percent, 3.5 percent, 2.5
percent, and 2.5 percent of his or her monthly income for improved water services.3 The
willingness to pay per cubic meter of water tended to increase for lower piped water consumption
levels from: US$0.05 for 180 liters per capita per day (lcd) (house connection), US$0.08 for 110
lcd (yard connection), and US$0.09 for 70 lcd (standpost). The willingness to pay for hand pump
water was estimated at US$0.07 per cubic meter.4
10.    The "Water Vendors" survey found that households who relied on water vendors were
paying US$0.57 per cubic meter of water delivered to their premises and consuming on average
44 liters (of unsafe water) per capita per day.5 The same survey assessed the willingness of the
population to pay for improved quality of delivered water. The majority of the households were
willing to pay US$0.17 per 100 liters (or 10 liter per capita per day) of good quality water for
drinking and cooking purpose delivered at the household's door,6 which is equivalent to 7
percent of their monthly income.
11.    Given the high willingness to pay for improved water services revealed by the surveyed
households, ranging from 2.5 percent to 7.0 percent of their monthly income when up to 90
percent of their cash income is spent on food, it seems logical to question the realism of the
payments for water. There is evidence which suggest that households indeed spend large shares
of their monthly income buying water from vendors: between zero and 7.7 percent during the
cold season and between 5.5 percent and 12 percent during the warm season. Therefore, the
above results were assumed to be the true willingness-to pay, and then a sensitivity analysis was
carried out to test how these values affects the economic rate of return of the investment.
I      Field observations shown that the majority of households had a pretty good notion of the amount of water
they used and how much they would use under hypothetical circumstances. Most of the level of services
offered in the survey existed in the community or its surroundings.
2      During the latest socio-economic survey carried out in February 1996, the average household income in the
project area was estimated at US$44 per month. This figure was used to estimate the monthly payments for
water services.
These figures reflect the one-time payment amortized at 10 percent real rate of interest over 25 years and
the monthly contribution revealed by the surveyed households for improved water services as a share of the
average household monthly income, which at the time of the survey (November 1994) was estimated at
US$25.
Surveyed rural households revealed greater satisfaction with hand pumps than with piped water supplies
(constant quality through the year and greater reliability). The high willingness to pay is a reflection of it.
Water consumption with an individual hand pump is estimated at 95 liter per capita.
The "Water Vendors" survey found that water consumption levels on a per capita basis were 54 liters
during the warm season and 36 liters during the cold season. The amount paid to vendors remained
constant year round.
No statistically significant relationship at 15 percent or better level was found between the price paid for
vendor's water and the quality of the water.



ANNEX K                                - 162-
11.   Another interesting finding of the "Water Vendor" survey was that households who can
not afford to buy water from vendors made on a monthly basis 24 trips of 2 km each (both ways)
to collect 1.6 cubic meters of water. Households spent 720 hours per year fetching 11 liter of
water per capita per day. If 75 percent of the household time could be used for productive
activities valued at the minimum labor wage of Sum 400 per month or US$0.56 per day, the
economic cost of having to fetch water was estimated at US$3.1 per month7 or US$1.94 per
cubic meter.
12.   The above results were used to estimate water demand curves for the various types of
households in the project area. In this particular case, the water demand relationship was
assumed to be of a linear functional form, and defined by two known points: PI and Q, in the
without project situation (for example, the price of water charged for vendor water and the
household's consumption at that price) and P2 and Q2 in the with-project situation (for example,
the willingness to pay for improved water services and the household's consumption at the stated
price). Given that the value of the economic benefits will change with a different functional form
of the demand curve, a log-linear relationship for example, this assumption was included in the
sensitivity analysis.

1.    13.   Among domestic beneficiaries a distinction was made between those households
who would be connected to a water network, those who would depend on the use of
desalinized water from standposts, and those who would use hand pumps. Table K2 shows
the situation "without project" and the situation "with project" assumed to be faced by each

14.    Benefits of the project accruing to each type of domestic beneficiary were estimated as
follows. In the case of households that were without access to piped water services but would
depend on the public network, four benefits were identified: (a) the cost savings of not having to
buy water from vendors; (b) the cost savings of not having to spend time fetching water; (c) the
cost savings of not having to boil water; and (d) the consumer surplus on the increased quantity
of water used. For households already connected to the piped water supply network but suffering
from a 30 percent suppressed demand and inadequacies of the existing system (e.g., intermittent
services, uncertain or no pressure, pollution because of back-siphonage, almost non-existent
services to outlying households), two benefits were estimated: (a) the cost savings of not having



ANNEX K                                    -164 -
to boil water; and (b) consumer surplus on the increased consumption and increased reliability
and quality of the service.8
15.   For the case of rural households that were depending on unsafe water sources and would
gain access to hand pumps, two benefits were identified: (a) the cost savings of having to spend
less time in water collection; and (b) the consumer surplus on the increased consumption. For
those rural households that shared one hand pump among nine or more families, two benefits
were estimated: (a) the cost savings of having to spend less time collecting water because the
distance from the house to the hand pump would be reduced; and (b) the consumer surplus on
increased water consumption since the project would increase the ratio of hand pumps to
households from 1:9 to 1:5, e.g., the number of people depending on one hand pump would go
down, allowing consumption to increase.9
16.   Consumer surplus, and the cost savings of not having to buy water from venders were
derived directly from the demand curve. In the case of a urban household that would connect to
the piped water system through a house connection, for example, these benefits were estimated
as follows. In the "without project situation," the household was assumed to be buying 44 liters
per capita per day from water vendors at US$0.56 per cubic meter In the "with project situation,"
all household's members would use 162 liters per day (180 lcd without demand management)
and would pay at US$0.05 per cubic meter. The monthly cost savings accruing to each member
of the household were estimated at US$0.52 [44*30/1000*0.56*0.7]. 0 "The consumer surplus
is given by the area under the demand function between Q1 and Q2, as shown in Figure KI, and
represented by area C. This was estimated at US$0.81  per month  [(0.56+0.05)*(162-
44)*30/2/1000*0.75]12
17.   The cost savings of not having to boil water for drinking purpose were estimated as
follows. The various socio-economic surveys revealed that between 70 percent and 80 percent of
the interviewed households boiled water to cope with its poor quality. The project would reduce
this chronic problem by providing better quality water. The economic cost of fuel associated with
boiling 5 liter per person per day was estimated at US$0.22 per person per month
[5*365*200*0.042/4000/12*0.7].3 The base case assumes that 70 percent of the beneficiary
8      The analysis assumes that because the water system is plagued with reliability, quality problems, low

9      No cost savings based on not having to boil water were expected for those rural households using hand

10    Thirty percent of the price paid to vendors was assumed to be an economic rent.
For those households that in the "without project" situation were assumed to collect 11 liters per capita per
day from open sources and to have an opportunity cost of time of US$1.94 per cubic meter, the monthly
cost savings were estimated at US$0.45 [ 11 *30/1 000*1.94*0.7]. Only 70 percent of the opportunity cost of
time spent on water fetching was included in the calculation.
12     Given the uncertainty surrounding the linear form of the demand curve, the calculated consumer surplus
was reduced by 25 percent.
13    It was assumed that to boil 1 liters of water for 3 minutes some 200 kcal are needed. One kilogram of coal,
which economic cost was estimated at US$0.042 per kilogram, provides 4000 kcal.

except for those using hand pumps would stop doing so and each would save US$0.16
[US$0.22*0.7] as a result of the project.

19.   The economic analysis has assumed that the current distribution of piped water services
would prevail in the future. In urban areas this distribution was found to be as follows: 50 percent
with house connection, 40 percent with yard connections, and 10 percent with standpipes. In
rural areas, the distribution was assessed as follows: 20 percent with house connections, 40
percent with yard connections, and 40 percent with standpipes.
20.   Non-domestic consumers would also benefit from the project by increasing consumption.
The economic benefit was estimated as the average incremental cost of developing an alternative
water supply. In this case, the alternative supply is desalination of brackish water. The average
incremental cost of building a reverse osmosis plant of 150 m3 per day of capacity was estimated
at US$0.40/m3 (Sum 14.5 per cubic meter).
21.   Rehabilitation of the distribution networks, together with the demand management and
loss reduction program and the institutional strengthening component would allow reducing the
consumption of domestic consumers by 10 points by the year 2005 and to reduce unaccounted-
for water from by 10 and 15 points by the year 2005. As shown in Figure K2, the increased
consumption would be met from the reduction in water losses. Physical losses were estimated at
30 percent of total production in 1995. The level of unaccounted-for water is expected to be
around 45 percent. The benefits of the project would be to reduce the average operating cost per
cubic meter of water delivered by about 20 percent (from US$0.35 to US$0.28).
14   In the case of standpost and hand pumps, it was assumed that a fraction of the implicit cost incurred by
households in fetching water would continue in the "with project" situation.

22.   In the case of sanitation improvements, the starting point to estimate the benefits was the
"Social Needs Assessment" survey, which found that 50 percent or more of households would
pay US$5.9 as a one-time cash contribution (or 24 percent of their average monthly income) for
building an improved latrine and make monthly contributions of US$0.40 for maintaining it (in
November 1994 prices). The equivalent monthly contribution was estimated at 1.8 percent of
household income.
23.   A recent survey undertaken by the consultants implementing the rural sanitation pilot
projects, however, revealed a much higher willingness to pay for improved rural sanitation
facilities. Surveyed households shown a readiness to spend 45 percent of their monthly income
or US$19.80 as a one-time cash contribution for having installed on their premises an improved
latrine. The economic analysis has included this latest field observation and assumed that the
monthly benefit for improved rural sanitation conditions was equivalent to 2.0 percent of
household income or US$0.15 per person in February 1996 prices.
24.   This low willingness to pay for improved on-site facilities captures only part of the health
benefits associated with improved sanitation conditions. Interviewed households appeared to be
not well informned about the health risks associated with the degradation in sanitary conditions
around their houses'15. Therefore, using the average willingness to pay as a proxy for the value to
the user of having a cleaner environment would underestimate the social value of the project.
25.   The base case scenario takes into account the benefits to be yielded to users, which may
not reflect the total health benefits, from readily available clean water and improved sanitation
practices. The use of a social weight associated with private users' benefit seems appropriate in
some cases, e.g., improved sanitation.
26.   Table K4 summaries the net economic benefits for the various types of beneficiaries used
in the base case scenario.

conditions does not capture the medical costs and the burden on the medical systemns.

27.   Economic Rate of Return. The analysis assumed an average project economic life of 30
years after construction. The replacement costs of those components with a shorter life span such
as the pumps and motors for the pumping stations, and the filters and the pumps for the hand
pumps, were included in the cost stream. Although the life span of the pipelines and distribution
networks is about 40 years, no residual values were included in the calculation. Cost and benefit
streams were calculated for the "without project" situation and the "with project" situation.
28.   The various subcomponents of the proposed project including the technical assistance
were packaged together to define 10 individual schemes, 6 water schemes in Karakalpakstan, 3
individual schemes in Khorezm and 1 sanitation scheme in both regions. An economic analysis
of each individual subproject was performed. Table K5 lists the subprojects by ranking order of
IERR. The subproject for on-site sanitation showed the lowest IERR (2.7 percent). The highest
(31.5 percent) corresponded to the improvement of rural piped water supplies in Karakalpakstan.
The overall economic rate of return and the benefit cost ratio at a discount rate of 10 percent for
the water investments were estimated at 17.9 percent and 1.86 percent in the base case scenario.
Detailed calculations are presented in Tables K6 to K1 8.
29.   Some remarks are in order regarding the subcomponent with an economic rate of return
of less than 10 percent: improved sanitation. First, a social weight associated with private
benefits derived from on-site sanitation equal to 1.5 times (e.g. 1.5 times private benefit) will
make the IERR of the subproject rise to 10 percent. Second, a health cost greater than US$1.0 per
capita year will also make this subproject, economically viable. These seem to be reasonable
values, which lead to conclude that the subproject should be undertaken.

-182 -                               ANNEX K
30.   Sensitivity Analysis. Several risks that would jeopardize the successful implementation of
the project and effect its economic return were identified. They included: (a) reduction in benefits
because waste and losses would not reduce to the levels proposed under this project, reduction in
production costs do no materialize, assumptions regarding cost savings of not having to boil water
and not having to buy water from vendors are over-optimistic, and time savings of not having to
fetch water are less than expected; (b) increase in costs because of unexpected expenditures, and
higher operations and maintenance (O&M) costs; and (c) a lag in benefits because of construction
delays.
31.   Two sensitivity analyses were carried out for the proposed project. The first one was a
detenninistic one, and assessed the impacts on the economic rate of return of: (a) an increase in
investment costs of 15 percent above contingencies already included in the base case; (b) a
decrease in benefits of 15 percent; and (c) a one-year delay in beginning the implementing the
project. The results of the deterministic analysis were as shown in Table K19.
Table K19: Results of a Deterministic Sensitivity Analysis
Scenario                      Internal Economic Rate of Return
Karakalpakstan     Khorezm           Both
Base Case                            17 6%            18.2%           17.9%
Investment costs increase by 15%     15.2%            16.2%           15.7%
Benefits decrease by 15%             10.9%            12.9%           11.9%
Project delays by one year           15.4%            16.1%           15.7%
32.   The second analysis is a risk-based sensitivity analysis. Given the high degree of
uncertainty surrounding the data used in the evaluation, it was considered appropriate to carry out a
risk-based sensitive analysis using the @RISK simulation packages, which uses a Montecarlo
probabilistic approach. Table K20 shows the assumptions used to estimate the probability density
function for the calculating the distribution curve for the IERR. The results of the risk-based
analysis are summarized in Table K21.
Table K20: Uncertainty in Parameters Included in the Sensitivity Analysis
Parameter                    Min Value   Most Likely   Max Value
Value
Willingness to pay increases by                  -15%         0%         +15%
Consumer Surplus                                  50%        75%          100%
Unaccounted-for water targets increase by         -5%         0%          +5%
Economic rent in vending activities               20%        30%          40%
Population boiling water                          20%        30%           40%
Consumer surplus obtained with existing piped supplies  60%  70%           80%
Consumer surplus obtained by hand pump users      60%        70%           80%
Demand management target                           5%         10%          15%
Capital costs increase by                        -10%         0%         +10%
O&M costs increase by                            -10%         0%         +10%
Value of time in urban areas fraction of minimum wage  80%   90%          100%
Value of time in rural areas fraction of minimum wage  60%   70%           80%
Note: the distribution is assumed to be triangular.

Health and Hygiene Project, Helvetas, August 1996.
Final Report, Phase II: Community Based Pilot Demonstration Rural Sanitation, and Health
and Hygiene Project, Helvetas, February 1997.

Project, Uzbek Social Science Network, July 1995.

11.   JICA Final Report, October 1996.
